<div id="review_526005241" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/526005241" />
    <a title="Catie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1886642-catie"><img alt="Catie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385169623p2/1886642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/526005241?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catie" href="/user/show/1886642-catie">Catie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781441769107" href="/book/show/8966514-a-good-man-is-hard-to-find-and-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Catie by: </span>
      Flannery's parents, indirectly.
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer526005241" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6921678081628646251">I enjoy all kinds of writing. I like the simple, breezy writing that’s entertaining and takes very little effort to understand. I like the dense, loaded writing that takes weeks of thought and discussion to fully unpack. The remarkable thing about the writing of Flannery O’Connor is that it somehow seems to encompass that entire spectrum. <br/><br/>Her writing is without a doubt easy to digest, but I would never in a million years call it simple. Reading her prose feels less like reading and more like hav</span>
  <span id="freeText6921678081628646251" style="display:none">I enjoy all kinds of writing. I like the simple, breezy writing that’s entertaining and takes very little effort to understand. I like the dense, loaded writing that takes weeks of thought and discussion to fully unpack. The remarkable thing about the writing of Flannery O’Connor is that it somehow seems to encompass that entire spectrum. <br /><br />Her writing is without a doubt easy to digest, but I would never in a million years call it simple. Reading her prose feels less like reading and more like having her thoughts and imagery mainlined into my brain with no translation necessary. And yet, she also packs a massive punch of history and depth into her stories – a depth of meaning that, for me, sometimes didn’t become fully apparent until I’d thought about these stories (and mostly about these characters) for days. She has this incredible, consummate control over her words that I’ve rarely (if ever) seen. She manages to be succinct, yet descriptive; bare, yet three-dimensional; meaningful in a fraction of the words it would take a lesser writer. Her characters are so three-dimensional it’s like they spring up in your head, fully alive. <br /><br />And oh boy, does she write some reprehensible characters. Never before has an author so quickly succeeded in making me not only hate her characters, but anticipate their downfall with a sort of sadistic glee. Her characters are good, upstanding country people who feel reassured by their habits and place in the world. And it’s oh so easy to sit on high and judge them for their willful ignorance – to feel holier, more worldly. Meanwhile, they’re feeling holier than everyone else in their community – set apart. But by the end, Flannery O’Connor shows you (more like punches you in the face with the fact) that the joke’s actually on you and her characters both. The world of Flannery O’Connor is a brutal, violent place where no one is ever safe or set apart – especially not her readers and ESPECIALLY not her characters.<br /><br />One of my favorite stories in this collection is <i>
  <a href="http://faculty.weber.edu/jyoung/English%206710/Good%20Country%20People.pdf" rel="nofollow noopener">Good Country People</a>
</i>, about an upstanding southern mother and her thirty year old, “crippled” daughter who holds a Ph.D in philosophy but who still lives at home and is viewed as a child due to her condition:<br /><br /><i>“Nothing is perfect. This was one of Mrs. Hopewell’s favorite sayings. Another was: that is life! And still another, the most important, was: well, other people have their opinions too. She would make these statements, usually at the table, in a tone of gentle insistence as if no one held them but her, and the large hulking Joy, whose constant outrage had obliterated every expression from her face, would stare just a little to the side of her, her eyes icy blue, with the look of someone who had achieved blindness by an act of will and means to keep it.”</i><br /><br />With that tiny paragraph, she says more about their relationship than I could ever say in any summary. The story follows Joy, (who has rechristened herself “Hulga” in an act of rebellion) as she decides to condescend to have a fling with a simple, travelling bible salesman, gets more than she bargained for, and stumbles over her own true core beliefs in the process. My other favorites were <i><a href="http://pegasus.cc.ucf.edu/~surette/goodman.html" rel="nofollow noopener">A Good Man is Hard to Find</a>, A Stroke of Good Fortune, A Circle in the Fire,</i> and <i>The Displaced Person</i>. (A couple of these are available for free online so I've linked to them here.)<br /><br />Narrator Marguerite Gavin is excellent as always. She nails all of the different Southern accents...and tackles more N-bombs than I'm guessing she's ever said in her entire life. I know there's been some claim that these stories are racist, but I would wholeheartedly disagree. Rather, these stories are <i>about</i> racism and <i>about</i> racist characters - one never gets the sense that Flannery O'Connor holds these beliefs herself or is complicit.<br /><br /><u>Perfect Musical Pairing</u><br /><br />Tammy Wynette – <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=km3O3G99OtU" rel="nofollow noopener">Count Your Blessings (Instead of Sheep)</a><br /><br />This book is the muddy green of a baptismal river and the rusty brown of old blood…It’s the starched white of new handkerchiefs and the tattered yellow of a family bible…and it’s soundtrack is Tammy Wynette.<br /><br />Also seen at <a href="http://www.thereadventurer.com/1/post/2013/02/adult-audiobook-review-a-good-man-is-hard-to-find-by-flannery-oconnor.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Readventurer</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6921678081628646251" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3071230669" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3071230669" />
    <a title="Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32041201-howard"><img alt="Howard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587918150p2/32041201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3071230669?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Howard" href="/user/show/32041201-howard">Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156364652" href="/book/show/432341.A_Good_Man_Is_Hard_to_Find_and_Other_Stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=american-south">american-south</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3071230669" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4286000988344323645"><b>SARDONIC</b><br/>Sardonic is an adjective resorted to by many a critic in their efforts to explain Flannery O’Connor and her writing. According to Merriam-Webster the word can be defined as “disdainfully or skeptically humorous; derisively mocking.” It is a perfect description of the writer and her work.<br/><br/>Here is an example of O’Connor’s sardonic humor:<br/><br/><i>Everywhere I go, I’m asked if the universities stifle writers. My opinion is that they don’t stifle enough of them. There’s many a best seller that could ha</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4286000988344323645" style="display:none"><b>SARDONIC</b><br />Sardonic is an adjective resorted to by many a critic in their efforts to explain Flannery O’Connor and her writing. According to Merriam-Webster the word can be defined as “disdainfully or skeptically humorous; derisively mocking.” It is a perfect description of the writer and her work.<br /><br />Here is an example of O’Connor’s sardonic humor:<br /><br /><i>Everywhere I go, I’m asked if the universities stifle writers. My opinion is that they don’t stifle enough of them. There’s many a best seller that could have been prevented by a good teacher.</i><br /><br />One of the stories in the <i>A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories</i> collection is <i>The Life You Save May Be Your Own</i>. She sold the film rights to the story, thereby waiving any chance to influence what might eventually appear on the screen. In 1957, it was produced as an episode on a TV anthology series known as the “Schlitz Playhouse of Stars.” <br /> <br />For some reason, the character Tom T. Shiflet’s name was changed to Tom T. Triplet. I point that out only because Shiftlet – as in shifty or shiftless – was a name chosen by O’Connor as shorthand for a character who cons a widow, marries and deserts her deaf mute daughter, and drives off in the widow's car.<br /><br />Maybe the reason that the film’s producers changed the name is because Gene Kelly was cast in the role. Yes, that Gene Kelly, the wholesome, exuberant, singin’ and dancin’ in the rain, Gene Kelly, was chosen to portray the amoral drifting grifter. So, maybe not only the name of the character was changed; perhaps he was given some redeeming qualities; or he and the deaf mute daughter lived happily ever after. I don’t know for I have never watched the episode nor know of anyone who has even heard of it.<br /><br />O’Connor’s reaction:<br /><br />“A story I sold will star a tap dancer….The punishment always fits the crime.”<br /><br />O'Connor was confident that her story would be butchered beyond recognition. She wrote that “Mr. Shiftlet and the idiot daughter will no doubt go off in a Chrysler and live happily ever after. Anyway…while they make hash out of my story, Regina (her mother) and me will make ice in the new refrigerator.”<br /><br />The refrigerator was purchased with the money she received for the film rights to the story. So it wasn’t a total loss.<br /><br /><br /><b>“I don’t deserve any credit for turning the other cheek as my tongue is always in it.” – Flannery O’Connor</b><br /><br /><br /><b>GROTESQUE</b><br /><b>“Anything that comes out of the South is going to be called grotesque by the northern reader, unless it is grotesque, in which case it is going to be realistic.” – Flannery O’Connor</b><br /><br />Grotesque, defined as fanciful, bizarre; absurdly incongruous; departing markedly from the natural, the expected, or the typical, is another adjective used by critics in their descriptions of O’Connor’s stories.<br /><br />One would have to agree that they most assuredly depart from the natural or the expected or the typical, and that they are sometimes fanciful or bizarre or incongruous, and, yes, absurdly so, and in some stories all of the above is true.<br /><br />How else could one characterize a story in which a criminal murders a family that is on vacation or a Bible salesman who seduces a lonely college-educated woman and then steals her artificial leg? Those are two of the stories included in <i>A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories.</i><br /><br />O’Connor described the collection as “nine stories about original sin with my compliments.” A number of the characters are “small-minded, ignorant, vain, parochial and smug widows,” self-righteous people who are quick to criticize and denounce the shortcomings that they see in others, but are blind to their own, but in the end always receive their comeuppance. (Some critics speculate that their model was her widowed mother, who confessed that she didn’t like or understand the stories that her daughter wrote.)<br /><br />Something bad is bound to happen in her stories, but even that awareness does not prepare the reader for the fate of the widowed grandmother in the title story. The climax is so shocking that Rod Serling would not have touched that story with a ten foot pole.<br /><br /><b>“I come from a family where the only emotion respectable to show is irritation. In some this tendency produces hives, in others literature, in me both.” – Flannery O’Connor</b><br /><br />Finally a bit of trivia:<br /><br />1) Did you know that Tommy Lee Jones wrote his thesis at Harvard on Flannery O’Connor?<br /><br />2) Did you know Tommy Lee Jones was a Harvard alumnus?<br /><br />3) Bonus: Who was Jones’ Harvard roommate?</span>
  <a data-text-id="4286000988344323645" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2467550636" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2467550636" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2467550636?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2018-read">2018-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=short">short</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=race">race</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=southern">southern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=linked">linked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2467550636" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12655354326115589321">All ten of the stories in this anthology are depressing, macabre and grotesque. Their strength lies in their vivid description of life in the South for the many poor, uneducated and disadvantaged.<br/><br/>Flannery O’Connor (1925-1964) writes in the Southern Gothic Style. Disabilities, sanity, race, religion and social inequalities are scrutinized in a cold detached manner. Not a pinch of sentimentality is to be found. Her writing is remarkably vivid. First published in 1953, the collection opens a window</span>
  <span id="freeText12655354326115589321" style="display:none">All ten of the stories in this anthology are depressing, macabre and grotesque. Their strength lies in their vivid description of life in the South for the many poor, uneducated and disadvantaged.<br /><br />Flannery O’Connor (1925-1964) writes in the Southern Gothic Style. Disabilities, sanity, race, religion and social inequalities are scrutinized in a cold detached manner. Not a pinch of sentimentality is to be found. Her writing is remarkably vivid. First published in 1953, the collection opens a window on life in the South during the first half of the 20th century. One can draw parallels with life there today<br /><br />I did not “enjoy” reading this. I found it excruciatingly difficult, but I feel this was O’Connor’s intention. She <b>meant</b> to shake her readers, and she does. I am glad I read the stories, but I will not be picking up more. I understand d her message and this is enough.<br /><br />Below, each of the stories are reviewed in the order they appear in the book. Read THE ARTIFICIAL NIGGER first. I think it is the best. If you cannot stomach this, do not even try the rest. It is the only one with a pinch, mind you only a pinch, of something good that lifts you above the gloom of the rest. I recommend reading them with an interval of several days between each. <br /><br />A GOOD MAN IS HARD TO FIND<br /> (1 star)<br />Horrible, but realistic. The “Misfit”, a dangerous criminal, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[kills <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> a family of six traveling in their car. Each is <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[killed <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> one by one. Gruesome and violent and what is the message? <br /><br /> THE RIVER <br />(1 star)<br />A little boy of 4 or 5 years is taken by his babysitter, Mrs. Cronin, to a preacher who is to wash away the child’s pain in the river, the “River of Life, of God and of God’s Blood”. The preacher is to pray for the child’s “sick” mother too, in reality, she has only a hangover. He is baptized, but has said his name is Bevel. This is not true; his being the name of the preacher! The boy walks the next day alone to the river and tries to baptize himself, but <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[he drowns <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <u>Again, I do not understand the message</u>. Does God help or doesn’t he? Is it a blessing that the boy <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[is released from his awful existence <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>? <br /> <br /><br /> THE LIFE YOU SAVE MAY BE YOUR OWN <br />(1 star)<br />A disabled, poor tramp stops at a house where a widowed old lady and her retarded daughter live. The mother encourages the tramp to marry her daughter and live with them, and so help in repairing the house and doing chores. She gives <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ them money for a honeymoon. He takes off in the woman’s repaired car, leaves the daughter along the way and drives off to Mobile, Alabama <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Again, this says nothing to me. <br /><br /> A STROKE OF GOOD FORTUNE <br />(2 stars)<br />An exhausted woman Is attempting to pull herself up flights and flights of stairs. At each landing she talks with the tenant there—a teacher, a secretary, to a chiropractor who tells her <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[she is pregnant <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The trip up the stairs is excruciatingly long and difficult. She realizes<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ she is probably pregnant, which she does not want to be <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>! I just wanted the story to end. I was glad we did not have to get to the top of the stairs. Her misery was palpable, but what was the story’s point other than to describe the plight of a distraught, struggling and suffering woman?! I felt her pain, but do not like the story. I appreciate the writing though.<br /><br /> A TEMPLE OF THE HOLY GHOST <br />(3 stars)<br />Two 14-year-old girls at a convent spend the weekend at the house of their 12-year-old second cousin. The two girls are in their young teens and all they think about is boys and the mystery of sex. The younger child is curious too. The older girls go to a fair and tell the younger what they saw—<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[a human being that was both woman and man <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The girls go back to school and we are told the fair was closed down. What the story does well is draw young adolescent girls’ queries about “the facts of life” in a convent setting. Their ideas are confused and jumbled up with religious explanations. Adults do not explain sex in clear text. The details vividly capture how adolescents try to make sense of what no one explains. What is described may be accurate, but <u>as with all the stories so far, absolutely none of the characters are appealing</u>. Should the world be described consistently in such dark tones?! <br /><br /> THE ARTIFICIAL NIGGER <br />(5 stars)<br />Finally, a story that I thought was really great. It is about a grandfather and grandson that take a train ride into Atlanta. They are poor, uneducated Whites. The description of both the train ride and what they experienced in Atlanta is magnificent. The two get lost in the black part of town. They are frightened and so are we. Heart-pounding. we watch as the grandfather, in a horrible manner, lets the grandson down. Despair is drawn in the “artificial nigger” statue of the story, the statue of the title, and in the lives of <b>both</b> Blacks and Whites. It is in this story that O’Connor grabs and holds the reader best, emotionally pulling the reader in. Morals, ethics and religion can do overlap well in this story. There is an emotional pull in this story that is missing in the others <b>and</b>, for once, there is a positive element to the story. Taking the trip into town brought about something good. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[They were now both happy to live where they did <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Thank goodness I did not give up on the stories too soon! Now, for the first time, I look forward to the stories that lie ahead. <br /><br /> A CIRCLE IN THE FIRE<br />(With distance from the story, I am willing to give it 3 stars.)<br />Three teenage boys visit Mrs. Cope, her daughter and Mrs. Pritchard, who works for her. One of the boys had lived there as a child. The atmosphere is dark and threatening. The boys are in control; the women cannot make them leave. Mrs. Pritchard regales in calamitous stories. Mrs. Cope copes by stressing all one has to be thankful for. The boys <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[start a fire and the reader understands that the house and property will burn <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The tragedy of the story is that despite Mrs. Cope thankful words, we know she knows that her situation is dire. This is made evident in the description of how Mrs. Cope holds her body as the tale nears its end. Her very survival is dependent on the mercy of the three boys. There is not one chance in a hundred that the boys will show mercy. Mrs. Pritchard’s calamitous stories <b>are</b> the reality of these women’s lives. The tone of the story is menacing and frightening. The strength of the story lies in its depiction of life in the South. <u>As in all of the stories, I struggled with the author’s unclear use of pronouns</u>. <br /><br /> A LATE ENCOUNTER WITH THE ENEMY<br /> (2 stars)<br />A 62-year-old granddaughter and her 104-year-old grandfather are together at the granddaughter’s graduation ceremony. She is to finally get a graduation diploma. The grandfather is up on the stage in a wheel chair in his general’s uniform. His age has made him both mentally and physically disabled. The story is a mix of sad and funny—mostly sad in its depiction of an elderly person’s physical decrepitude and loss of sanity. He <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[dies <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> up on the stage. The story may be accurate in its depiction, but certainly not pleasant. O’Connor focuses only on difficult situations. The delusional old man’s cracks about women may perhaps bring a smile to your face. <u>The humor is not to my taste</u>. At least the story was not as long and drawn out as the others.<br /><br /> GOOD COUNTRY PEOPLE <br />(1 star)<br />Good country people are NOT “good country people” is the moral of this story. A so-called bible salesman <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[seduces <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> a thirty -two-year-old, unloved spinster daughter with an artificial leg and no prospects for marriage. “That is life” and “Nothing is perfect” are the words said to convince one’s self that life without happiness, a life filled with degradation and loneliness, without human dignity or goodness, is not so bad. This story draws the worst in man to an extreme. <u>The story is way too long, as so many of the others have been</u>.<br /><br /><br /> THE DISPLACED PERSON <br />(1 star)<br />The displaced person in this story is a Pole. He has survived life in a concentration camp. He is diligent and hard working. This is why Blacks and the poor White despise him. At the end, the Blacks, the white workers and the farm owner <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[let a tractor run over the displaced person, allowing him to be killed <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>!! He has disturbed life’s equilibrium. The employees <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[leave, the woman becomes sick and the priest is the only one who visits <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The message? Foreigners are never liked because they are different. The story is depressing and too long.<br /><br /><br />A free online link is found at Gutenberg: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.boyd.k12.ky.us/userfiles/447/Classes/28660/A%20Good%20Man%20Is%20Hard%20To%20Find.pdf">http://www.boyd.k12.ky.us/userfiles/4...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="12655354326115589321" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3026880839?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="RJ from the LBC" class="user" itemprop="url" name="RJ from the LBC" href="/user/show/131730-rj-from-the-lbc">RJ from the LBC</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571322855" href="/book/show/32144622-a-good-man-is-hard-to-find">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3026880839" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer595553650700897533">O'Connor plumbs the depth of the human soul and readers are often uncomfortable with what they find within, yet there are also frequent moments of humor as well. She was prolific prior to her early demise, and many of her most notable works are her short stories. This collection is bookended by two of her best short stories although there isn't a dud in the batch. As usual, ratings for individual stories are below along with song lyrics that might be diverting or insightful. <br/><br/>A Good Man is Hard t</span>
  <span id="freeText595553650700897533" style="display:none">O'Connor plumbs the depth of the human soul and readers are often uncomfortable with what they find within, yet there are also frequent moments of humor as well. She was prolific prior to her early demise, and many of her most notable works are her short stories. This collection is bookended by two of her best short stories although there isn't a dud in the batch. As usual, ratings for individual stories are below along with song lyrics that might be diverting or insightful. <br /><br />A Good Man is Hard to Find - 5/5 - I give in to sin because you have to make this life livable <br />The River - 4/5 - take me to the water, drop me in the river <br />The Life You Save May Be Your Own - 4/5 - do you wanna get married, or run away? <br />A Stroke of Good Fortune - 3/5 - I can't believe what God has done<br />A Temple of the Holy Ghost - 4/5 - you Catholic girls start much too late<br />The Artificial Nigger - 4/5 - the millions of faces but still I'm alone<br />A Circle in the Fire - 4/5 - we didn't start the fire<br />A Late Encounter With the Enemy - 4/5 - I can't remember anything, can't tell if this is true or dream<br />Good Country People - 4/5 - well a simple kind of life never did me no harm<br />The Displaced Person -5/5 - nowhere man, the world is at your command</span>
  <a data-text-id="595553650700897533" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1750042466" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1750042466" />
    <a title="Paul Haspel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40256937-paul-haspel"><img alt="Paul Haspel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425160128p2/40256937.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1750042466?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Haspel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Haspel" href="/user/show/40256937-paul-haspel">Paul Haspel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/11367079-a-good-man-is-hard-to-find-and-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40256937-paul-haspel?shelf=southern-lit">southern-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1750042466" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11769607884142908378">A good story is *not* hard to find in this classic collection by one of the greatest Southern U.S. writers. Working at the height of her powers, Flannery O’Connor sets down, in an unforgettable manner, her unique and distinctive sense of the life of her region, in the ten stories that make up her 1955 collection <i>A Good Man Is Hard to Find and Other Stories</i>.<br/><br/>Flannery O’Connor is nowadays so taken-for-granted as one of the pre-eminent writers of the Southern Literary Renaissance that it is easy to </span>
  <span id="freeText11769607884142908378" style="display:none">A good story is *not* hard to find in this classic collection by one of the greatest Southern U.S. writers. Working at the height of her powers, Flannery O’Connor sets down, in an unforgettable manner, her unique and distinctive sense of the life of her region, in the ten stories that make up her 1955 collection <i>A Good Man Is Hard to Find and Other Stories</i>.<br /><br />Flannery O’Connor is nowadays so taken-for-granted as one of the pre-eminent writers of the Southern Literary Renaissance that it is easy to forget the ways in which she broke with the prevailing values and mores of the American South. She was a woman in a male-dominated region. She was a devout Roman Catholic in a part of the United States where evangelical Protestantism dominated everyday life and dictated social codes. <br /><br />And, diagnosed at age 27 with the systemic lupus erythematosus that took her life 12 years later, she knew what it was to be a person with a disability in a region that looked at “different-ness” of any kind with distrust, or worse. All of these factors from her life no doubt contributed to the way in which she created characters who are often “misfits” of one kind or another – characters who often end up in situations that are violent and/or horrifying, and who yet have the potential to experience some sort of moment of grace.<br /><br />The title story, “A Good Man Is Hard to Find,” is one of those stories that is often described as “heavily anthologized” – meaning, in other words, that you would have to look hard to find a college-level literature anthology that does not contain this story. Yet the reason why it is so heavily anthologized is that it is, purely and simply, a great story, and one that is strongly characteristic of O’Connor’s literary sensibility and thematic concerns.<br /><br />The story also shows the ways in which O’Connor’s South is different from that of, say, William Faulkner. O’Connor’s is an urbanizing and suburbanizing South, as shown in this story of an Atlanta-area family who, en route to their planned Florida vacation, have to drive a long way just to get out of the city (an experience with which many I-285/Perimeter drivers of today could no doubt identify). <br /><br />The story’s protagonist, the grandmother of the family, is an embodiment of various Southern prejudices – always ready with a racist joke against African Americans, and obsessed with her status as a “lady” of presumably higher-than-someone-else social station. And the story’s title comes from a moment when the family stops at a barbecue restaurant and the restaurant’s proprietor, one “Red Sammy,” says, “A good man is hard to find….Everything is getting terrible. I remember the day you could go off and leave your screen door unlatched. Not no more” (p. 16). Red Sammy’s nostalgic idealization of the past captures the falsity of thinking that characterizes the grandmother, and so many of O’Connor’s characters.<br /><br />But the grandmother’s race and class prejudices do not protect her when some bad decisions on her part cause the family to have a deadly run-in with an escaped convict known as “the Misfit,” who laments that he did not live in Biblical times and therefore was not there to see whether or not Jesus of Nazareth could truly raise the dead. Only when she is on the verge of losing everything she holds dear does the grandmother, nominally a devout Protestant, truly realize her kinship with all of fallen humankind, as she reaches out toward the murderous Misfit and says, “Why, you’re one of my babies. You’re one of my own children!” (p. 29)<br /><br />Another canonical O’Connor story from this collection is one whose title I am going to refer to simply as “The Artificial” – because the n-word (yes, <i>the</i> n-word) is part of the title. “The Artificial” shares the Atlanta setting of the story "A Good Man Is Hard to Find"; and like that title story of this collection, "The Artificial" also presents a thoroughly unpleasant grandparent as protagonist - one Mr. Head, who is taking his grandson Nelson on a train trip into the city. Each constantly tries to pull rank on the other – Nelson because he has visited Atlanta before (though only as a very small child), and Mr. Head because, unlike Nelson, he has seen African Americans (the last African-American resident of Mr. Head’s county was driven out 12 years prior). <br /><br />The trip goes drastically wrong, and Mr. Head shows himself capable of a terrible act of abandonment against Nelson; but just when it seems as though this trip to the big city can only end in disaster, the two encounter, in an affluent suburban area of Atlanta, a racist lawn-ornament statue of an African American – an “artificial Negro.” After all that they have been through, and all of the hostility that they have expressed toward each other, Mr. Head and Nelson “stood gazing at the artificial Negro as if they were faced with some great mystery, some monument to another’s victory that brought them together in their common defeat. They could both feel it dissolving their differences like an action of mercy” (p. 128).<br /><br />“Good Country People,” another of the more canonical stories in this volume, takes its title from the declaration by Mrs. Hopewell, the owner of a Georgia farm, that the Freemans, a couple she has hired to help her run the farm, “were not trash. They were good country people” (p. 170) - another instance of O'Connor revealing the deep-seated class prejudices of the South. But the true focus of the story is Mrs. Hopewell’s daughter Joy, who has renamed herself “Hulga” because it is the ugliest name she can think of. <br /><br />Joy/Hulga, in response to her mother’s relentless cheerfulness, has recast herself as a sort of philosophical nihilist; she is an avid reader of Heidegger (interesting choice, that, as Heidegger was a supporter of Nazism before, during, and even after the Second World War), and she may remind you of that student you knew in your sophomore year at college who thought it was oh-so-cool to believe in nothing. Yet Joy/Hulga gets her comeuppance at the hands of a traveling Bible salesman named Manley Pointer (great name), who, once he has gotten the better of Joy/Hulga, dismisses Joy/Hulga’s fashionable nihilism with a devastating rejoinder: “[Y]ou ain’t so smart. I been believing in nothing ever since I was born!” (p. 195)<br /><br />The other stories may not be as well known, outside the realm of Southern literature, but each one has its own special power. “The River,” the story of a boy who takes Biblical accounts of total-immersion baptism quite literally, generates almost unbearable suspense as the main character walks into a river to self-baptize: “He only saw the river, shimmering reddish yellow, and bounded into it with his shoes and his coat on and took a gulp. He swallowed some and spit the rest out and then he stood there in water up to his chest and looked around him….He intended not to fool with preachers any more but to Baptize himself, and to keep on going this time until he found the Kingdom of Christ in the river” (p. 51).<br /><br /> “A Late Encounter with the Enemy,” the story of a superannuated Confederate veteran who has become an object of celebration by a Civil War-nostalgic Southern culture, is of interest chiefly for its scrutiny of the way in which many Americans from both North and South, during the time around the Civil War Centennial of 1961-65, wanted to celebrate the heroism of Civil War soldiers without addressing the issues over which the Civil War was fought. Yet the “General Sash” who is the main character of the story turns out not to be a general at all; “He had not actually been a general in that war. He had probably been a foot soldier; he didn’t remember what he had been; in fact, he didn’t remember that war at all” (p. 157).<br /><br />And “The Displaced Person,” the story that concludes this collection, may be best understood in terms of the post-World War II milieu within which O’Connor was composing. “Displaced person” may not be such a familiar term nowadays, but the term “displaced persons’ camp” would have been quite familiar to people who knew of many such camps being established for Holocaust survivors after the end of the Second World War. In the case of this story, the title character, a Pole and a Holocaust survivor, comes to post-World War II Georgia and, through his hard work and his knowledge of agricultural life, distinguishes himself as a foreman on a Georgia farm; but when his egalitarian ways bring him to question the South’s unjust racial norms, the stage is set for tragedy.<br /><br />I re-read <i>A Good Man Is Hard to Find and Other Stories</i> in the context of two trips to Georgia earlier this year. In towns like Brunswick and Hinesville and Midway, I could imagine characters from O'Connor's stories coming to life before me -- Bailey scowling behind the wheel of his car at a stoplight, while the grandmother from the collection's title story tells him that his travel directions are all wrong; Nelson and Mr. Head arguing on the sidewalk in front of a hardware store; Joy/Hulga complaining that the town library contains no books by Heidegger; General Sash's descendants at the county courthouse, protesting plans to take down a Confederate-soldier monument. O'Connor's stories, for all their elements that may seem odd or distorted, are that vivid in terms of the fictive world that they create. <br /><br />This short-story collection covers a lot of ground in a mere 251 pages. One need not share O’Connor’s religious beliefs to be moved and impressed by the way in which O’Connor sets forth her insights regarding the human search for meaning and truth in life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11769607884142908378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17579742370446405978">The New York Times, in a review of O'Connor's stories, referred to her as an American Guy de Maupassant. This is an apt description. O'Connor's stories paint a dark yet spot-on picture of the human condition. She takes the <i>quaint</i> out of southern living and shines a spotlight on the ignorance and prejudice with a razor-sharp and truly wicked sense of humor. Reading her stories left me amazed by her literary ability yet also a bit nauseated. What depresses me the most is that current events seem t</span>
  <span id="freeText17579742370446405978" style="display:none">The New York Times, in a review of O'Connor's stories, referred to her as an American Guy de Maupassant. This is an apt description. O'Connor's stories paint a dark yet spot-on picture of the human condition. She takes the <i>quaint</i> out of southern living and shines a spotlight on the ignorance and prejudice with a razor-sharp and truly wicked sense of humor. Reading her stories left me amazed by her literary ability yet also a bit nauseated. What depresses me the most is that current events seem to bear out O'Connor's less than flattering assessment of human nature. Nothing else can explain Donald Trump's success in the polls. </span>

<div id="review_7148776" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7148776" />
    <a title="Rochelle Torke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/436271-rochelle-torke"><img alt="Rochelle Torke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335476292p2/436271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7148776?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rochelle Torke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rochelle Torke" href="/user/show/436271-rochelle-torke">Rochelle Torke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer15259563206275159639">Oh good lord. Someone said she made the south seem even creepier than it already was and i agree with a shudder. She can create a character in five words that you will recognize instantly way, way down in your cerebellum--or maybe somewhere in your gut--and it will live there inside you forever. I think she is the unmatched master of the short story form. And don't get me wrong, you will laugh at times while reading. But the</span>
  <span id="freeText15259563206275159639" style="display:none">Oh good lord. Someone said she made the south seem even creepier than it already was and i agree with a shudder. And my experience is that you can never really shake off these stories. She can create a character in five words that you will recognize instantly way, way down in your cerebellum--or maybe somewhere in your gut--and it will live there inside you forever. I think she is the unmatched master of the short story form. And don't get me wrong, you will laugh at times while reading. But then later in the evening, you'll feel this chill pass over you and you'll just want to make a run for it--probably all the way north, to Canada or someplace.</span>

<div id="review_2258906924" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2258906924" />
    <a title="Alison Hardtmann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28105228-alison-hardtmann"><img alt="Alison Hardtmann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542141808p2/28105228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2258906924?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alison Hardtmann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alison Hardtmann" href="/user/show/28105228-alison-hardtmann">Alison Hardtmann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156364652" href="/book/show/432341.A_Good_Man_Is_Hard_to_Find_and_Other_Stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28105228-alison-hardtmann?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2258906924" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5310751894845339306">I read the first short story in this collection by Flannery O'Connor and sat back, astonished. If you haven't read <i>A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories</i>, grab a copy right now and read the title story. You'll know then exactly where you stand with O'Connor's stories after that first one has slammed into you like a hammer to the back of the head.<br/><br/>In <i>A Good Man is Hard to Find</i>, a family prepares for and sets off for a vacation in Florida. Even Grandma, who has much to say about how much bett</span>
  <span id="freeText5310751894845339306" style="display:none">I read the first short story in this collection by Flannery O'Connor and sat back, astonished. If you haven't read <i>A Good Man is Hard to Find and Other Stories</i>, grab a copy right now and read the title story. You'll know then exactly where you stand with O'Connor's stories after that first one has slammed into you like a hammer to the back of the head.<br /><br />In <i>A Good Man is Hard to Find</i>, a family prepares for and sets off for a vacation in Florida. Even Grandma, who has much to say about how much better it would be to visit family in east Tennessee and how the trip might be dangerous, what with escaped felons and other perils, comes along to narrate the ride. And off they go, stopping at bbq joints for lunch and staring at the sights outside the car windows. It begins as one sort of story and ends as quite another and it's one of the most brilliant things I've ever read. <br /><br />Each story is finely honed and reads as surprisingly contemporary, for all it's written about a rural South that is long gone. O'Connor is insightful and cutting and unafraid to allow the worst to happen. There is a dark comedy underlying her work and a deep understanding of people, albeit a somewhat grim one. People in this collection die. They're drowned, or shot, or simple run over. They look into someone else's eyes and see how badly they've misjudged things. They are callous and cruel and lonely and disillusioned. Their hopes are inevitably dashed, usually because of their own flaws. There's so much packed into each of these tightly written stories that each feels like an entire world. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5310751894845339306" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raul Bimenyimana" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana">Raul Bimenyimana</a>

I don't think I've encountered a white writer that wrote of quotidian racism and the strange bond it creates between racist individuals with such insight since I read Lessing. This was so good and so well written, one captivating story after the other and now I can't wait to read her novels and the collection of essays. </span>

<div id="review_1943776609" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1943776609" />
    <a title="Clif Hostetler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/793473-clif-hostetler"><img alt="Clif Hostetler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593302055p2/793473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1943776609?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clif Hostetler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clif Hostetler" href="/user/show/793473-clif-hostetler">Clif Hostetler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781441769107" href="/book/show/8966514-a-good-man-is-hard-to-find-and-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/793473-clif-hostetler?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1943776609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13534487367161923631">Flannery O'Connor died in 1964 at the young age of 39, but she managed in her short life to produce a collection of literature that has been the subject of enduring praise from literary critics. It was because of this reputation I decided it was time to checkout this collection of short stories. This is the first work of hers that I've read (or listened to on audio). <br/><br/>Most of the stories in this book take place in the rural part of the state of Georgia in the first half of the twentieth century (</span>
  <span id="freeText13534487367161923631" style="display:none">Flannery O'Connor died in 1964 at the young age of 39, but she managed in her short life to produce a collection of literature that has been the subject of enduring praise from literary critics. It was because of this reputation I decided it was time to checkout this collection of short stories. This is the first work of hers that I've read (or listened to on audio). <br /><br />Most of the stories in this book take place in the rural part of the state of Georgia in the first half of the twentieth century (probably 40s after WWII). The rural people are generally portrayed as poor and exhibiting little appreciation for people from outside their own community. They are often portrayed as not being very intelligent, and at times it seems the author was going out of her way to make the people of the rural south appear foolishly ignorant. The author was a native of the southern USA (from Georgia) so it's not clear to me whether she's purposely writing to make fun of the South or whether she's simply writing descriptively about the world with which she is familiar.<br /><br />Many of the stories in this book have dark and shocking endings. At the end of almost all the stories my reaction was to think, "This is the end? Huh!" In other words, not all dilemmas portrayed in the story have been resolved by the end, and the reader is left wondering what was going to happen next. <br /><br />The last story titled <u>"The Displaced Person"</u>, did provide a wrap-up of events toward the end which gave the reader a sense of an ending. This story is my favorite of this book's collection probably because it touches of the subject of immigration which is a current hot-button issue today.<br /><br />Many of the stories contain irony and can be interpreted through multiple layers of meanings, some of which include religious overtones. Wikipedia and other on-line sources contain articles describing and analyzing each of the short stories in this book. I'll not discuss the stories in detail other than to provide the short descriptions below.<br /><br />One of the reasons I don't enjoy reading collections of short stories is because when I'm finished they all sort of conflate in a blur of memory. To help overcome this problem I have prepared the following very short summaries of each of the stories as reminders to myself as to what story goes with which title. The links provided are to the respective Wikipedia articles for the stories. The numerical numbers in parentheses provide the story's location in the audio edition of the book. Those numbers can give the prospective reader an indication of the approximate time required to read that story.<br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Good_Man_Is_Hard_to_Find_(short_story)" rel="nofollow noopener">A Good Man Is Hard to Find</a>
</u> (00:00:000—00:39:09) A family travels from Georgia to Florida for a summer vacation and end up in a single car accident on a remote road. <a href="https://www.enotes.com/homework-help/what-kind-person-misfit-211455" rel="nofollow noopener">"The Misfit"</a> and two friends come along and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[kill them. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_River_(short_story)" rel="nofollow noopener">The River</a>
</u> (00:39:09—01:23:00) A 4-year-old boy is brought to a Christian revival meeting by his babysitter. The boy is baptized in the river. He later returns to the river to re-experience the events of the preceding day and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[drowns while attempting to find the Kingdom of Christ that he understood to be under the river. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Life_You_Save_May_Be_Your_Own" rel="nofollow noopener">The Life You Save May Be Your Own</a>
</u> (01:23:00—01:50:40) A transient <a href="https://www.enotes.com/homework-help/what-do-you-think-name-shiftlet-means-what-does-322232" rel="nofollow noopener">shiftlet</a> visits a farm, marries daughter, "borrows" car to take bride on honeymoon, and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[abandons the daughter at a cafe. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Stroke_of_Good_Fortune" rel="nofollow noopener">A Stroke of Good Fortune</a>
</u> (01:50:40—02:21:00) A woman who discovers to her disappointment and disbelief that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[she is pregnant. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Temple_of_the_Holy_Ghost" rel="nofollow noopener">A Temple of the Holy Ghost</a>
</u> (02:21:00—02:51:32) A pair of 14-year-old cousins who are Roman Catholic convent school girls visit home of a 12-year girl. The older cousins go to a fair and see a hermaphrodite. Upon returning the cousins describe what they saw to the younger girl who <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[misunderstands their explanation and comes to the conclusion that the body is in fact a temple of the holy ghost. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Artificial_Nigger" rel="nofollow noopener">The Artificial Nigger</a>
</u> (02:51:32—03:43:17) A grandfather takes his grandson to Atlanta to see the big city. They get lost and are lucky to find the train depot so they can return home. They decide to never visit the city again. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The story gets its title from an incident near the end of the story during which they pass by a figurine of a black lawn jockey which prompts the grandfather to say, "They ain't got enough real ones here. They got to have an artificial one." <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Circle_in_the_Fire" rel="nofollow noopener">A Circle in the Fire</a>
</u> (03:43:17—04:28:20) A woman owner of a farm in the South is visited by three teenage boys. They won't leave and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[they set fire to the place. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Late_Encounter_with_the_Enemy" rel="nofollow noopener">A Late Encounter with the Enemy</a>
</u> (04:28:20—04:55:05) 104-year-old veteran of the American Civil War attends his daughter's graduation ceremony and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[dies. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Good_Country_People" rel="nofollow noopener">Good Country People</a>
</u> (04:55:05—05:48:07) Mrs. Hopewell lives on a farm with her 32-year old, one-legged daughter Joy. A Bible salesman shows up and persuades Joy to enter a barn loft in which he manages to <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[steal and run off with her prosthetic leg. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><u>
  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Displaced_Person" rel="nofollow noopener">The Displaced Person</a>
</u> (05:48:07—07:36:00) Mrs. McIntyre operated a farm in Georgia. A Polish refugee begins to work for her, and she decides he is so efficient and such a hard worker that she can lay off all her negro hired hands as well as the white foreman. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The Polish refugee is killed by an "accident." Everyone leaves and Mrs. McIntyre suffers nervous breakdown. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Rk+Z477uOHh+rQ+0yZRq6ixHywGXYWhh2j2bvnATEZdpYhg3T5zjM03ZFqZnj6U6NRqlzHjqdbKEZQpNI88qZw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('n+Js9N/WDf66NwAiCSyVWkUFeNHB2bivwkI8jvPHvBiwz+0gLqTWtYlDGTCnN1qKXFgWHC5SpXycGq19oBuH6A==')})
  }

Enlever sa perruque, sa robe de soie, rouler ses bas sur ses chevilles : ses gestes ressemblent à un arrachement. Bientôt, celle <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15682014431203102035\" style=\"display:none\">Sur le parking d’un supermarché, dans une petite ville de province, une femme se démaquille méticuleusement, tristement. Enlever sa perruque, sa robe de soie, rouler ses bas sur ses chevilles : ses gestes ressemblent à un arrachement. Bientôt, celle qui, à peine une heure auparavant, volait quelques instants de joie et dansait à corps perdu sera devenue méconnaissable. Laurent, en tenue de sport, a remis de l’ordre dans sa voiture et dissimulé dans le coffre la mallette contenant ses habits de fête. Il s’apprête à retrouver femme et enfants pour le dîner. Petit garçon, Laurent passait des heures enfermé dans la penderie de sa mère, détestait l’atmosphère virile et la puanteur des vestiaires après les matchs de foot. Puis il a grandi, a rencontré Solange au lycée, il y a vingt ans déjà. Leur complicité a été immédiate, ils se sont mariés, Thomas et Claire sont nés, ils se sont endettés pour acheter leur maison. Solange prenait les initiatives, Laurent les accueillait avec sérénité. Jusqu’à ce que surviennent d’insupportables douleurs, jusqu’à ce qu’il ne puisse plus réfréner ses envies incontrôlables de toucher de la soie, et que la femme en lui se manifeste impérieusement. De tout cela, il n’a rien dit à Solange. Sa vie va basculer quand, à la faveur de trois jours solitaires, il se travestit pour la première fois chez eux. À son retour, Solange trouve un cheveu blond… Léonor de Récondo va alors suivre ses personnages sur le chemin d’une transformation radicale. Car la découverte de Solange conforte Laurent dans sa certitude : il lui faut laisser exister la femme qu’il a toujours été. Et convaincre son entourage de l’accepter. La détermination de Laurent, le désarroi de Solange, les réactions contrastées des enfants – Claire a treize ans, Thomas seize –, l’incrédulité des collègues de travail : l’écrivain accompagne au plus près de leurs émotions ceux dont la vie est bouleversée.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15682014431203102035\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n It is 1971, a year after the Biafran War, and Nigeria is under military rule—though the politics of the state matter less than those of her home to Enitan Taiwo, an eleven-year-old girl tired of waiting for school to start. Will her mother, who has become deeply religious since the death of Taiwo\'s brother, allow her friendship with the new girl next door, the brash and beautiful Sheri Bakare? Everything Good Will Come charts the fate of these two African girls, one born of privilege and the other, a lower class &quot;half-caste&quot;; one who is prepared to manipulate the traditional system while the other attempts to defy it.\n\nWritten in the voice of Enitan, the novel traces this unusual friendship into their adult lives, against the backdrop of tragedy, family strife, and a war-torn Nigeria. In the end, Everything Good Will Come is Enitan\'s story; one of a fiercely intelligent, strong young woman coming of age in a culture that still insists on feminine submission. Enitan bucks the familial and political systems until she is confronted with the one desire too precious to forfeit in the name of personal freedom: her desire for a child. Everything Good Will Come evokes the sights and smells of Africa while imparting a wise and universal story of love, friendship, prejudice, survival, politics, and the cost of divided Ghana Must G<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6281647528978727723\" style=\"display:none\">Kweku Sai is dead. A renowned surgeon and failed husband, he succumbs suddenly at dawn outside his home in suburban Accra. The news of Kweku’s death sends a ripple around the world, bringing together the family he abandoned years before. Ghana Must Go is their story. Electric, exhilarating, beautifully crafted, Ghana Must Go is a testament to the transformative power of unconditional love, from a debut novelist of extraordinary talent.  Moving with great elegance through time and place, Ghana Must Go charts the Sais’ circuitous journey to one another. In the wake of Kweku’s death, his children gather in Ghana at their enigmatic mother’s new home. The eldest son and his wife; the mysterious, beautiful twins; the baby sister, now a young woman: each carries secrets of his own. What is revealed in their coming together is the story of how they came apart: the hearts broken, the lies told, the crimes committed in the name of love. Splintered, alone, each navigates his pain, believing that what has been lost can never be recovered—until, in Ghana, a new way forward, a new family, begins to emerge.Ghana Must Go is at once a portrait of a modern family, and an exploration of the importance of where we come from to who we are. In a sweeping narrative that takes us from Accra to Lagos to London to New York, Ghana Must Go teaches that the truths we speak can heal the wounds we hide.<\/span>\n  <a Out of the blue, a telegram arrives from Christine’s rich American aunt inviting her to a resort in the Swiss Alps. Christine is immediately swept up into a world of inconceivable wealth and unleashed desire. She feels herself utterly transformed: nothing is impossible. But then, abruptly, her aunt cuts her loose. Christine returns to the post office, where yes, nothing will ever be the same again.\n\nChristine meets Ferdinand, a bitter war veteran and disappointed architect, who works construction jobs when he can get them. They are drawn to each other, even as they are crushed by a sense of deprivation, of anger and shame. Work, politics, love, sex: everything is impossible for them. Life is meaningless, unless, through one desperate and decisive act, they can secretly remake their world from within.\n\nCinderella meets Bonnie and Clyde in Zweig’s haunting and hard-as-nails novel, completed during the 1930s, as he was driven by the Nazis into exile, but left unpublished at the time of his death.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3731638984146309742"><b>Flannery O'Connor</b> was born in Savannah, Georgia, in 1925. When she died at the age of thirty-nine, America lost one of its most gifted writers at the height of her powers. O’Connor wrote two novels, Wise Blood (1952) and The Violent Bear It Away (1960), and two story collections, A Good Man Is Hard to Find (1955) and Everything That Rises Must Converge (1964). Her Complete Stories, published posth</span>
  <span id="freeText3731638984146309742" style="display:none"><b>Flannery O'Connor</b> was born in Savannah, Georgia, in 1925. When she died at the age of thirty-nine, America lost one of its most gifted writers at the height of her powers. O’Connor wrote two novels, Wise Blood (1952) and The Violent Bear It Away (1960), and two story collections, A Good Man Is Hard to Find (1955) and Everything That Rises Must Converge (1964). Her Complete Stories, published posthumously in 1972, won the National Book Award that year, and in a 2009 online poll it was voted as the best book to have won the award in the contest’s 60-year history. Her essays were published in Mystery and Manners (1969) and her letters in The Habit of Being (1979). In 1988 the Library of America published her Collected Works; she was the first postwar writer to be so honored. O’Connor was educated at the Georgia State College for Women, studied writing at the Iowa Writers’ Workshop, and wrote much of Wise Blood at the Yaddo artists’ colony in upstate New York. She lived most of her adult life on her family’s ancestral farm, Andalusia, outside Milledgeville, Georgia</span>
  <a data-text-id="3731638984146309742" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
